We measure photometry using T-PHOT and perform spectral energy distribution fitting using EAzY and BAGPIPES. Of the 207 candidates for which we could successfully measure (or place limits on) Spitzer fluxes, 23 were demoted to likely z < 4. Among the high-z candidates, we find intrinsic stellar masses between 1 \u00d7 10<SUP>6</SUP>M<SUB>\u2299</SUB> and 4 \u00d7 10<SUP>9</SUP>M<SUB>\u2299</SUB>, and rest-frame UV absolute magnitudes between -22.6 and -14.5 mag. While our sample is mostly comprised of ${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* } 1$ <!-- --> galaxies, it extends to ${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\sim 2$ <!-- --> . Our sample spans \u223c4 orders of magnitude in stellar mass and star formation rates, and exhibits ages that range from maximally young to maximally old. We highlight 11 z \u2265 6.5 galaxies with detections in Spitzer/IRAC imaging, several of which show evidence for some combination of evolved stellar populations, large contributions of nebular emission lines, and/or dust. Among these is PLCKG287+32-2013, one of the brightest z \u223c 7 candidates known (AB mag 24.9 at 1.6 \u03bcm) with a Spitzer 3.6 \u03bcm flux excess suggesting strong [O III] + H-\u03b2 emission (\u223c1000 \u00c5 rest-frame equivalent width). We discuss the possible uses and limits of our sample and present a public catalog of Hubble + Spitzer photometry along with physical property estimates for all objects in the sample. Because of their apparent brightnesses, high redshifts, and variety of stellar populations, these objects are excellent targets for follow-up with the James Webb Space Telescope.", "doi": <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F910%2F2%2F135\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/910/2/135</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <p>We present constraints on the physical properties (including stellar mass, age, and star formation rate) of 207 6 \u2272 <i>z</i> \u2272 8 galaxy candidates from the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS) and Spitzer-RELICS surveys. We measure photometry using T-PHOT and perform spectral energy distribution fitting using EA<i>z</i>Y and BAGPIPES. Of the 207 candidates for which we could successfully measure (or place limits on) Spitzer fluxes, 23 were demoted to likely <i>z</i> &lt; 4. Among the high-<i>z</i> candidates, we find intrinsic stellar masses between 1 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>6</sup><i>M</i><sub>\u2299</sub> and 4 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>9</sup><i>M</i><sub>\u2299</sub>, and rest-frame UV absolute magnitudes between \u221222.6 and \u221214.5 mag. While our sample is mostly comprised of <img alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1$\" src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn1.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=hiA%2BgjzNAQjsgBL0eH3PHv3j8Kk%3D\"></img> galaxies, it extends to <img alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\sim 2$\" src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn2.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6di8aFeswHzuRIfPQR3Ui6OfonE%3D\"></img>. Our sample spans \u223c4 orders of magnitude in stellar mass and star formation rates, and exhibits ages that range from maximally young to maximally old. We highlight 11 <i>z</i> \u2265 6.5 galaxies with detections in Spitzer/IRAC imaging, several of which show evidence for some combination of evolved stellar populations, large contributions of nebular emission lines, and/or dust. Among these is PLCKG287+32-2013, one of the brightest <i>z</i> \u223c 7 candidates known (AB mag 24.9 at 1.6 <i>\u03bc</i>m) with a Spitzer 3.6 <i>\u03bc</i>m flux excess suggesting strong [O <span style=\"font-variant: small-caps\">iii</span>] + H-<i>\u03b2</i> emission (\u223c1000 \u00c5 rest-frame equivalent width). We discuss the possible uses and limits of our sample and present a public catalog of Hubble + Spitzer photometry along with physical property estimates for all objects in the sample. Because of their apparent brightnesses, high redshifts, and variety of stellar populations, these objects are excellent targets for follow-up with the James Webb Space Telescope.</p></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/910/2/135&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/abe533&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<div class=\"linked-articles linked-articles--issue-nav\">\n\t<div bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\"><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s1\" name=\"apjabe533s1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Properties of <em>z</em> \u2273 6 galaxies are interesting not only for piecing together the role of galaxies in reionization, the period of time in the universe when energetic photons ionized neutral hydrogen in the intergalactic medium (IGM), but also as the key building blocks of galaxy formation models. Galaxies in this epoch often reveal characteristics rarely seen in local galaxies. Average stellar properties of galaxies up to <em>z</em> \u223c 4\u20135 have been reasonably well characterized; with access to a wealth of information from multiwavelength observations of characteristic galaxies, such as rest-frame optical and infrared (IR) data from Keck and Herchel for <em>z</em> \u223c 2\u20133, and the Atacama Large Millimeter Array (ALMA) for <em>z</em> \u223c 1\u20135, we have detailed accounts of basic physical properties such as star formation rate (SFR), stellar mass, and age, as well as metal enrichment and dust content for galaxies in this regime (e.g., Duncan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib30\">2020</a>; Fudamoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib42\">2020</a>; Sanders et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib84\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib84\">2020</a>). The picture becomes much less clear at higher redshifts, where due to intrinsic faintness of distant galaxies, increased absorption by the IGM, and the difficulty of obtaining red enough data to break degeneracies, we often have to rely on broadband imaging data and spectral energy distribution (SED) fitting for this information.</p><p>Broadband photometry, and in particular, Spitzer/Infrared Array Camera (IRAC) fluxes play an important role in measuring the physical properties of galaxies at <em>z</em> \u2273 6 (see Brada\u010d <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib10\">2020</a> for a review). Because the rest-frame optical wavelengths are redshifted into the IR in this regime, Spitzer/IRAC 3.6 and 4.5<em> \u03bc</em>m ([3.6] and [4.5] hereafter) observations are necessary for constraints of stellar mass and age until the James Webb Space Telescope (JWST) is functional.</p><p>There have been a multitude of studies using Spitzer/IRAC fluxes to probe the rest-frame optical wavelengths of high-redshift galaxies. Notable examples include large surveys such as Hubble Frontier Fields (Merlin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib64\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib64\">2016</a>; Castellano et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib23\">2016</a>; Di Criscienzo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib29\">2017</a>; Lotz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib58\">2017</a>; Santini et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib85\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib85\">2017</a>; Shipley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib89\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib89\">2018</a>; Brada\u010d et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib12\">2019</a>), Hubble Ultra Deep Field (Eyles et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib34\">2005</a>; Yan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib99\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib99\">2005</a>; Labb\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib51\">2010</a>), GOODS Re-ionization Era wide-Area Treasury from Spitzer (Stefanon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib95\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib95\">2019</a>), Cluster Lensing and Supernova Survey with Hubble (Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib76\">2012</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib7\">2014</a>), and Spitzer UltRa-Faint Survey (SURFSUP; Brada\u010d et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib13\">2014</a>; Ryan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib80\">2014</a>; Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib44\">2016a</a>). Spitzer/IRAC [3.6] and [4.5] broadband imaging has been integral to facilitate and contextualize high-impact discoveries, such as evidence of evolved stellar populations at <em>z</em> &gt; 8 (Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib101\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib101\">2012</a>; Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib44\">2016a</a>; Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>; Mawatari et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib62\">2020</a>; Strait et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib96\">2020</a>), discovery of the most distant spectroscopically confirmed galaxy, Gnz11 (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib68\">2016</a>), discovery of the highest-redshift Ly<em>\u03b1</em> detection (Smit et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib91\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib91\">2015</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib103\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib103\">2015</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib69\">2015</a>; Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib78\">2016</a>; Jung et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib49\">2020</a>), measurement of nebular emission at <em>z</em> \u223c 4 (Shim et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib88\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib88\">2011</a>; Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib94\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib94\">2013</a>; Caputi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib20\">2017</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib9\">2016</a>; Faisst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib35\">2016</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib36\">2019</a>), and later measurement of nebular emission and stellar properties at <em>z</em> &gt; 5 (Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib78\">2016</a>; Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib45\">2016b</a>; Stefanon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib95\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib95\">2019</a>; De Barros et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib28\">2019</a>; Laporte et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib53\">2014</a>; Bridge et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib15\">2019</a>; Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>).</p><p>While the above surveys have established the groundwork for observations of bright and faint galaxies at <em>z</em> \u2273 6, open questions centered around characterizing high-redshift populations remain. Ionization field, dust content, metal enrichment, and ionizing photon production are some examples of still mostly unknown quantities for a \"normal\" galaxy at <em>z</em> \u2265 5.5. The answers to these unknowns will require significant spectroscopic follow-up time with existing and future telescopes.</p><p>In this work, we use Spitzer/IRAC observations to measure physical properties and identify the most interesting galaxies for future spectroscopic follow-up. Unique to this work is the use of gravitational lensing of a large number of galaxy clusters to probe the most apparently bright but perhaps intrinsically fainter high-redshift sources. The Reionization Cluster Lensing Survey (RELICS; PI Dan Coe) and companion survey Spitzer-RELICS (S-RELICS) were designed to characterize the population of galaxies at these redshifts, and to attempt to find bright and rare galaxies at these epochs. To this end, these surveys image 41 massive clusters with Hubble Space Telescope (HST) imaging data from RELICS for all 41 of these clusters, which was used to select 321 <em>z</em> \u2265 5.5 candidates (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib82\">2020</a>). Here, we add the S-RELICS Spitzer/IRAC imaging to further refine and characterize this sample.</p><p>The structure of the paper is as follows: we describe HST and Spitzer imaging data and photometry in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s2\">2</a>, our SED modeling procedure and calculation of stellar properties in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>, and lens models used for correction to relevant stellar properties in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s4\">4</a>. We present SED fitting results in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5\">5</a>, discuss possible future data in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s6\">6</a>, and we conclude in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s7\">7</a>. Throughout the paper, we will give magnitudes in the AB system (Oke <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib72\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib72\">1974</a>), and we assume a Lambda cold dark matter cosmology with <em>h</em> = 0.7, \u03a9<sub><em>m</em>\n            </sub> = 0.3, and \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.7. Equivalent widths (EWs) are quoted in the rest frame.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s2\" name=\"apjabe533s2\">2.\u00a0Observations and Photometry</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>All imaging data used for this analysis were obtained through a combination of RELICS and archival data. Each cluster was observed with HST and Spitzer and was reduced in a way that optimizes the search for high-<em>z</em> galaxies. Here we briefly summarize the observing strategy of the survey, but in-depth information about observations can be found on the RELICS website<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn1\">\n               23\n            </a></sup>\n             and in the RELICS overview paper (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib27\">2019</a>). Images of selected HST and bands and both Spitzer channels for the <em>z</em> \u2265 6.5, IRAC-detected sample are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f1\">1</a>. Select photometry is summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabe533t3\">3</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f1a_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=Mmu8TsA%2FYI7VtntUETYpeOwXkKw%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; The rightmost panel is the resulting delensed source in the source plane of the galaxy with a 0.1'' size bar for comparison.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f1b\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f1b_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=1KZGLOZiXXBun29JgPIBFtdUQ5U%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f1b\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f1b_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=eEQtu%2FG9%2BgxXs1pjOygH6dRhuJs%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><h3 id=\"apjabe533s2-1\" name=\"apjabe533s2-1\">2.1.\u00a0HST</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Each cluster was observed with two orbits of Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3)/IR imaging split among the F105W, F125W, F140W, and F160W filters, and three orbits of Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS) split among F435W, F606W, and F814W (minus archival optical imaging), for a total of 188 HST orbits. Several clusters had archival ACS and/or WFC3 imaging in other filters (F390W, F475W, F555W, F625W, F775W, F850LP, F110W) and some clusters that received additional data from a subsequent proposal. Most clusters reach approximate HST depths of \u223c27 mag (3<em>\u03c3</em>) in the ACS bands and \u223c26 mag (3<em>\u03c3</em>) in the WFC3 bands.</p><p>In this paper, we use the catalogs based on a detection image comprised of the 0.06'' pixel<sup>\u22121</sup> weighted stack of all WFC3/IR imaging (to optimize the search for high-<em>z</em> galaxies) described in Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib27\">2019</a>) and available on the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn2\">\n                  24\n               </a></sup>\n               \n            </p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s2-2\" name=\"apjabe533s2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Spitzer Data and Photometry</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Spitzer/IRAC images for all clusters come from S-RELICS (Spitzer-RELICS, PI Brada\u010d #12005, 13165, 13210, Director's Discretionary Time, PI Soifer #12123), for a total of over 1000 hr of exposure time, and additional archival data. 13 clusters that had promising <em>z</em> \u223c 8 targets received deeper data via a follow-up proposal, to reach a total of 30 hr exposure time per band (3<em>\u03c3</em> depth \u223c26 mag) in each channel. All clusters reach a total of 5 hr of exposure time (3<em>\u03c3</em> depth \u223c24 mag) in each of IRAC channels 1 and 2 ([3.6] and [4.5]). A complete accounting of Spitzer data can be found in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabe533t1\">1</a> and all shallow images are available on the InfraRed Science Archive.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn3\">\n                  25\n               </a></sup>\n                All raw data are available for download on the Spitzer Heritage Archive (SHA<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn4\">\n                  26\n               </a></sup>\n               ).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjabe533t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0Exposure Times and Programs of Spitzer Data</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533t1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533t1_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=5XKcOXBgy866QA2QA%2BMz7X2YFCQ%3D\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col></colgroup><thead><tr valign=\"top\"><th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Cluster</th><th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R. id=\"apjabe533t1fna\"></a><sup> \n                        a\n                     </sup>R.A.s and declinations correspond to cluster centers.<a name=\"apjabe533t1fnb\" id=\"apjabe533t1fnb\"></a><sup> \n                        b\n                     </sup>Program IDs included in our reduction. ID #12005, 13165, 14281, 13165, 13210, 14017: PI Maru\u0161a Brada\u010d. #12123: PI Tom Soifer, #60034, 90218 PI Eiichi Egami, #14253 PI Mauro Stefanon, #14242 PI Andra Stroe, #50096 PI Paul Martini, #70149 PI Felipe Menanteau, #83 PI George Rieke, #14130 PI Rychard J Bouwens, #17 PI Giovanni Fazio, #20740, 50726, 70063 PI Bradford P Holden, #80012 PI Mark Brodwin.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/910/2/135/suppdata/apjabe533t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/abe533\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533t1_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=5XKcOXBgy866QA2QA%2BMz7X2YFCQ%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s2-2-1\" name=\"apjabe533s2-2-1\">2.2.1.\u00a0Reduction</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>To reduce and mosaic Spitzer images, we closely follow the process described by Brada\u010d et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib13\">2014</a>) for the SURFSUP survey, which also consists of IRAC images used for a search of high-<em>z</em> galaxies. Briefly, we begin downloading the corrected-basic calibrated data (cBCD) from the SHA. The cBCDs have been processed by the IRAC pipeline to remove instrumental artifacts, and to calibrate into physical units (MJy sr<sup>\u22121</sup>). We apply additional mitigation measures using custom scripts; specifically, we correct for the warm-mission column pulldown (using bandcor_warm.c by M. Ashby) and muxstriping (using automuxstripe.pro by J. Surace) that often occurs when bright sources are present (these scripts are located on the IRAC Cookbook website<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn5\">\n                     27\n                  </a></sup>\n                  ). To create the mosaic images we use the MOsaicker and Point source EXtractor (MOPEX) command-line tools and largely follow the process described in the IRAC Cookbook for the COSMOS medium-deep data. The corrected frames are background-matched using the overlap.pl routine from MOPEX and then drizzle-combined into a mosaic using the mosaic.pl routine. The final mosaics have a pixel scale of 0.6'' pixel<sup>\u22121</sup> and a pixel fraction of 0.1. As a last step, we use the <tt>Tweakreg</tt> routine from <tt>DrizzlePac</tt>, which compares the positions of bright objects in Spitzer and HST images, to correct for any shifts in relative astrometry.</p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s2-2-2\" name=\"apjabe533s2-2-2\">2.2.2.\u00a0Flux Extraction and Error Analysis</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Intracluster light (ICL) subtraction, background subtraction, and flux extraction are done using T-PHOT (Merlin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib65\">2015</a>), designed to perform PSF-matched, prior-based, multiwavelength photometry on low-resolution imaging as described by Merlin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib65\">2015</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib64\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib64\">2016</a>). This is done by convolving cutouts from a high-resolution image (in the case of this work, HST/WFC3 F160W) using a low-resolution point-spread function (PSF) transformation kernel that matches the high-resolution image to the low-resolution (in our case, [3.6] and [4.5]) image. T-PHOT then fits a template to each source detected with HST and convolves the template with a PSF transformation kernel to match the resolution to that of the IRAC image. T-PHOT then solves for the solution where the model image created from the convolved HST image best matches the pixel values in the real IRAC image and outputs fluxes for each template. We use the F160W image and WFC3/IR total weighted segmentation map as the priors for T-PHOT. Because T-PHOT takes a template-fitting approach where all templates are solved for simultaneously, an approach designed for blank fields which have a zero mean background, we fit each high-<em>z</em> candidate separately, running T-PHOT on a small field of view (FOV) centered on the candidate, to account for the changing background and ICL in a cluster field. Within the 12'' (\u223c70 kpc for cluster redshift of <em>z</em> = 0.4) FOV on which we run T-PHOT, the background shows minimal variation: in most cases, where the object is far from the cluster center, we see no variation in background (i.e., the variation is centered on zero and random), while this can reach as high as \u223c20% where ICL is denser near the cluster center. For these reasons, and due to the fact that most of our galaxies are far from the cluster center where ICL is not dense, further ICL modeling and subtraction are not necessary in addition to subtracting background.</p><p>While T-PHOT is useful for predicting fluxes of objects with potential blending, it does not account for this blending in its output uncertainties. However, it calculates a covariance matrix that includes the covariance between each object and every other object in the image. This can be used to calculate a maximum covariance index (<em>R</em><sub>[3.6],[4.5]</sub>), which is the ratio of the covariance of an object with its closest or brightest neighbor and its own variance. If this value is higher than 1, the object is severely blended with a neighbor and caution should be taken interpreting fluxes. We report the <em>R</em><sub>[3.6],[4.5]</sub> values with the fluxes in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabe533t3\">3</a> and include them in the catalog for all sources in the sample.</p><p>In practice, T-PHOT does not work as well near bright sources and the cluster center, creating artifacts in the residual (see several objects in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f1\">1</a>). For this reason, we only include fluxes in this work for which we believe we are able to reliably extract Spitzer fluxes, meaning the residual on top of the high-<em>z</em> candidate is not a residual artifact due to a bright nearby source. To ensure T-PHOT is not underestimating flux uncertainties, we calculate our own statistical uncertainties for each source. In the residual image, we measure background levels 100 times within 3'' of each high-<em>z</em> candidate, avoiding artifacts from neighboring objects. The mean should fall close to zero, and we take the standard deviation as the error. In most cases, this error is smaller than the error reported by T-PHOT, however, in some cases it is larger. In those cases, we use the larger uncertainty.</p><p>The PSF and convolution kernel used to convolve prior HST images to the resolution of IRAC images are important for this process. We create a PSF by stacking point sources, identified with a Source Extractor (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib6\">1996</a>) run with the following parameters: DEBLEND_MINCONT = 10<sup>\u22127</sup>, MINAREA = 9, DETECT_THRESH = 2, and ANALYSIS_THRESH = 2. We select point sources using the stellar locus in <tt>FLUX</tt>_<tt>RADIUS</tt> versus <tt>MAG</tt>_<tt>AUTO</tt> space for every object in the IRAC image (not just in the HST FOV). After the point sources are selected, we further require that their axis ratio is &gt;0.9 and that they are sufficiently separated from neighbors to have a secure centroid. We recompute the PSF centroids by fitting a Gaussian profile to the inner profile (<em>r</em> &lt; 4 pixels), and align the point sources using sinc interpolation. At each phase we subtract the local background and normalize the flux of the point source to one. We sigma-clip average the masked, registered, normalized point sources and do one further background correction to ensure the convolutions with T-PHOT are flux conserving. Our stacked PSFs are consistent with the IRAC handbook<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn6\">\n                     28\n                  </a></sup>\n                  , and each of our clusters' PSFs contains at least 40 point sources per IRAC channel. In practice, the PSF convolution kernel needs to be \"sharpened\" to produce cleaner residuals. For each individual high-<em>z</em> candidate, we experiment with increasing and decreasing the weight of the core of the PSF to produce the cleanest residual. Most of the time, this means decreasing the weight of the core by a factor of 0.8\u20130.9.</p></div></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s3\" name=\"apjabe533s3\">3.\u00a0Estimating Galaxy Properties</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Throughout this work, we will refer to two separate methods for estimating physical properties of galaxies. Method A is our primary routine, which we use in order to compare to previous similar works (e.g., Huang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib45\">2016b</a>). We mainly refer to results from Method A in this work (e.g., the values in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabe533t4\">4</a>, and results in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f2\">2</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f5\">5</a>). Method B is a secondary routine, which we will use for comparison purposes in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f3\">3</a>, as well as in discussion of biases and uncertainties in our analysis (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3-3\">3.3</a>), and in the exploration of the six objects we discuss in detail in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5\">5</a>. We report results from both analyses in the attached catalog (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-2\">5.2</a>.)</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=vO6IytUc81J19HH%2FfZMnv2u3aEk%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; These are described in full in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f3b\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f3b_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=FYbnM%2BIU0O4VsH2etuEGsg7IXvc%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f3b\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f3b_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=e%2FIsOYUfL1ZRlfzpVkbiab471a8%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><h3 id=\"apjabe533s3-1\" name=\"apjabe533s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Method A</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In our primary SED fitting method, we first fit for redshift, and then use the resultant redshift posterior when fitting for other properties. We start by calculating colors from HST and IRAC imaging to estimate a redshift probability distribution function (PDF) for each source using the redshift estimation code Easy and Accurate Redshifts from Yale (EA<em>z</em>Y; Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib14\">2008</a>). This code compares the observed SEDs to a set of stellar population templates. Using linear combinations of a base set of templates from Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib17\">2003</a>, BC03) and no priors on magnitude, EA<em>z</em>Y performs <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> minimization on a user-defined redshift grid, which we define to range from <em>z</em> = 0.1\u201312 in linear steps of <em>\u03b4</em>\n               <em>z</em> = 0.1, and computes a PDF from the minimized <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> values. We use this PDF to calculate best-fit redshift and uncertainties, but opt for a slightly different process to calculate stellar properties.</p><p>To calculate stellar properties, we sample from the redshift PDF (we do not fix a redshift) and our photometry assuming a normal error distribution 1000 times, each time finding a best-fit template, again with EA<em>z</em>Y. In order to be able to extract physical properties associated with each template, we do not allow linear combination of templates during this stage of SED fitting. We use a set of \u223c2000 stellar population synthesis templates from the updated BC03 templates from 2016<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn7\">\n                  29\n               </a></sup>\n               , assuming the following: a Chabrier initial mass function (IMF; Chabrier <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib25\">2003</a>) between 0.1 and 100 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, a metallicity of 0.2 <em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, a constant star formation history (SFH), a Small Magellenic Cloud (SMC) dust law (Prevot et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib77\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib77\">1984</a>) with E<sub>stellar</sub>(B-V) = E<sub>nebular</sub>(B-V) with step sizes of \u0394E(B-V) = 0.05 for 0\u20130.5 mag and 0.1 for 0.5\u20131 mag. We allow the formation age (the age from when the first stars in the galaxy formed) to range from 10 Myr to the age of the universe at the redshift of the source. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3-3\">3.3</a>, we discuss the motivations behind these assumptions and the biases and uncertainties that come from our choices.</p><p>We also include nebular emission lines and continuum to account for the role these can have in contributing to broadband fluxes (Schaerer &amp; de Barros <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib86\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib86\">2010</a>; Smit et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib92\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib92\">2014</a>). To do this, we first calculate hydrogen recombination line strength following the relation from Leitherer &amp; Heckman (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib56\">1995</a>), scaling from integrated Lyman-continuum flux, and then follow the strengths determined with nebular line ratios by Anders &amp; Fritze-v. Alvensleben (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib5\">2003</a>). We also include Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, calculating expected strengths using the ratio with H-<em>\u03b1</em> and assuming a Case B recombination in Brocklehurst (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib16\">1971</a>) with a Ly-<em>\u03b1</em> escape of 20%.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s3-2\" name=\"apjabe533s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Method B</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>For our secondary method of estimating galaxy properties, we use the program Bayesian Analysis of Galaxies for Physical Inference and Parameter EStimation (BAGPIPES; Carnall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib22\">2018</a>). Instead of the default BC03 models, we implemented the Binary Population and Spectral Synthesis (BPASS v2.2.1; Eldridge &amp; Stanway <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib32\">2009</a>) templates, reprocessed to include nebular continuum and emission lines using the photoionization code <tt>CLOUDY</tt> c17.01 (Ferland et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib37\">2017</a>). BAGPIPES fits redshift in parallel with physical properties of galaxies using the MultiNest nested sampling algorithm (Feroz &amp; Hobson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib38\">2008</a>; Feroz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib39\">2009</a>; Feroz &amp; Skilling <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib40\">2013</a>) to navigate the multidimensional space of physical parameters. For this work, we implement an exponential SFR with flexibility to rise or decline at any rate, or stay constant:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjabe533eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533eqn1.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=cHhLPtAflY9l%2B5Ug1F2gZwR7114%3D\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\mathrm{SFR}(t)\\propto {e}^{-t/\\tau },\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n               </p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjabe533eqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533eqn2.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=8mcXVCeW4UOyLaAZ%2FSpaxYIkZm4%3D\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\tau =\\displaystyle \\frac{{t}_{\\mathrm{obs}}-{t}_{\\mathrm{form}}}{\\tan (R\\pi /2)},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>for a galaxy observed at time <em>t</em><sub>obs</sub> that began forming stars at time <em>t</em><sub>form</sub>. The parameter <em>R</em> dictates the rate of increase, ranging from \u22121 (maximally old burst), through negative fractional values (declining), 0 (constant), positive (increasing), to +1 (maximally young burst). We use the BPASS IMF <tt>imf135</tt>_<tt>300</tt>: Salpeter slope <em>\u03b1</em> = \u22122.35 between 0.5 and 300 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> and shallower <em>\u03b1</em> = \u22121.3 for lower mass stars 0.1\u20130.5 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Dust in BAGPIPES assumes a functional form described by the Calzetti law (Calzetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib18\">2000</a>), and we assign twice as much dust around H <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> regions as in the general ISM in the galaxy's first 10 Myr. We allow dust extinction to range from <em>A</em><sub><em>V</em>\n               </sub> = 0\u20133 magnitudes. We vary metallicity in log space from 0.005\u20135 <em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub> and allow ages of formation from 1 Myr to the age of the universe. These parameters are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabe533t2\">2</a>, and biases and uncertainties are discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3-3\">3.3</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjabe533t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey
