To use a different magnification value, use <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn103.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=FrD8iO%2BTHGefc3Rz2w2FIVNLAMg%3D\" alt=\"${M}_{(1600)}-2.5\\mathrm{log}({f}_{\\mu }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{(1600)}-2.5\\mathrm{log}({f}_{\\mu }</script></span></span>).<a name=\"apjabe533t4fni\" id=\"apjabe533t4fni\"></a><sup> \n                        i\n                     </sup>Absolute UV luminosity over characteristic luminosity, assuming median redshift and <em>M</em><sub>1600</sub>.</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/910/2/135/suppdata/apjabe533t4_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/abe533\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533t4_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=JCwGbJ%2FycvciNiwVe8YsZ5twzEs%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We reprocess the BPASS stellar continuum spectra using <tt>CLOUDY</tt>. Hydrogen and helium scale factors, as well as other model details, are listed in Eldridge &amp; Stanway (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib32\">2009</a>). We allow the ionization parameter log(<em>U</em>) to vary from \u22124 to \u22121, allowing a very large dynamic range of H-<em>\u03b2</em>[O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]<em>\u03bb</em>4959,5007 \u00c5 (EW can be &gt;10000 \u00c5 for the most extreme ionization conditions).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s3-3\" name=\"apjabe533s3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Biases and Uncertainties</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The biases and uncertainties associated with SED fitting have been well documented (e.g., Papovich et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib74\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib74\">2001</a>; Shapley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib87\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib87\">2001</a>; Lee et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib55\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib55\">2009</a>; Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib83\">2015</a>; Carnall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib22\">2018</a>; Leja et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib57\">2019</a>), and we further explore in this section considerations relevant to properties of our sample presented in this work.</p><h4 id=\"apjabe533s3-3-1\" name=\"apjabe533s3-3-1\">3.3.1.\u00a0SFHs</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>While there have been studies on the effect of SFH on inferred galaxy properties at lower redshift (<em>z</em> &lt; 2, e.g., Pacifici et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib73\">2016</a>; Carnall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib22\">2018</a>; Leja et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib57\">2019</a>; Lower et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib59\">2020</a>), few studies have focused on the very high-redshift regime we consider. The lower-redshift studies show that a bias can be introduced in stellar mass, SFR, and age from a choice of any parameterized SFH model (e.g., exponential or constant), due to the intrinsically bursty and stochastic nature of star formation. Specifically, Carnall et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib21\">2019</a>) found that stellar mass, SFR, and mass-weighted age vary with the choice of SFH model for mock photometry at <em>z</em> = 0 by at least 0.1, 0.3, and 0.2 dex, respectively. Furthermore, they find that, generally, photometric data alone cannot discriminate between SFH models. While the need to constrain SFHs may be somewhat alleviated at very high redshifts, as the universe has only had \u223c750 Myr to form stars at <em>z</em> \u223c 8, the very beginning of SFHs leave little imprint on a galaxy's SED, making it difficult to fully constrain. For this reason, we distinguish between formation age and mass-weighted age. We define formation age as <em>t</em><sub>obs</sub>\u2013<em>t</em><sub>form</sub>, or the age of a galaxy observed at time <em>t</em><sub>obs</sub> since it first started forming stars at time <em>t</em><sub>form</sub>. While this is a useful parameter for constraining the beginning of galaxy formation and reionization, we also report mass-weighted age, as this is a more robustly constrained parameter (Leja et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib57\">2019</a>). Mass-weighted age is defined as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjabe533eqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533eqn3.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=VAtieYS5oLd0Lf%2F2p0siib5cXVk%3D\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\mathrm{age}}_{{\\rm{MW}}}={t}_{\\mathrm{obs}}-{t}_{{\\rm{MW}}}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n                  </p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjabe533eqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533eqn4.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=pwnucg9wwfrlj1jw0%2FmU0FsbnKM%3D\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{t}_{{\\rm{MW}}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\int }_{{t}_{\\mathrm{form}}}^{{t}_{\\mathrm{obs}}}t\\ \\mathrm{SFR}(t)\\ {dt}}{{\\int }_{{t}_{\\mathrm{form}}}^{{t}_{\\mathrm{obs}}}\\mathrm{SFR}(t)\\ {dt}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n               </p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s3-3-2\" name=\"apjabe533s3-3-2\">3.3.2.\u00a0Metallicity and Dust</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In our Method A SED fitting template library, we assume a metallicity of 0.2 <em>Z</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. While there is evidence that this assumption is appropriate for at least some of our sample (Jones et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib47\">2020</a>), we still test the effects of these biases by fitting our data to templates using several dust laws and metallicities, and find that changing the dust law choice from SMC to Milky Way biases stellar masses high by \u223c0.5 dex, and that even large changes in metallicity introduce subdominant biases, &lt;0.1 dex. In Method B, we allow a range of metallicities and attenuations in order to remain agnostic to this issue.</p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s3-3-3\" name=\"apjabe533s3-3-3\">3.3.3.\u00a0Nebular Emission</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>It has been shown that emission lines can contribute significantly to broadband flux, and several recent studies have shown that these emission lines can be more extreme than previously thought. For example, the combination of H-<em>\u03b2</em> and [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] can sometimes be up to \u223c3000 \u00c5 in their rest-frame EW (Labb\u00e9 et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib52\">2013</a>; Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib41\">2013</a>; Smit et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib91\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib91\">2015</a>; Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>), and are regularly indirectly observed to be \u223c700 \u00c5 (Endsley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib33\">2021</a>). We have included nebular emission in our templates, as described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>. With our observational setup, the most relevant contaminating lines are [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] and H-<em>\u03b2</em>, as these are the likely strongest emission lines in our observed-frame window. These lines are observed in our templates from Method A to span a dynamic range from 0 to \u223c2000 \u00c5 in rest-frame EW. This range allows for extreme line-emitting galaxies to be identified for all but the rarest objects. However, there is a degeneracy in IRAC colors between extreme line-emitting galaxies at <em>z</em> \u223c 8 and those with a steep Balmer/D<sub>n</sub>(4000 \u00c5) break (hereafter, Balmer break), signifying an evolved stellar population. Since we allow for both in our templates in Method A, and allow ionization parameter log(U) to vary to extreme values in Method B, where [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] can have EWs upwards of 10000 \u00c5, our approach makes us agnostic to this issue. The degeneracy between a Balmer break and strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emitters, however, cannot be truly disentangled without secure measurements of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H<em>\u03b2</em> EW with JWST, and in some cases (<em>z</em> &gt; 9.1), indirectly with spectroscopic redshifts, to determine in which IRAC band the emission lines fall.</p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s3-3-4\" name=\"apjabe533s3-3-4\">3.3.4.\u00a0Binary Stars</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Lastly, it has been shown that the inclusion of binary stars in the creation of stellar population synthesis templates can influence the results (Eldridge et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib31\">2008</a>). For this reason, we use BPASS templates in Method B. We find that the change from BC03 to BPASS templates alone does not significantly change our results in terms of the average redshifts and stellar properties of our candidates, though considerable changes can be induced for individual candidates.</p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s3-3-5\" name=\"apjabe533s3-3-5\">3.3.5.\u00a0Differences in SED Fitting Methodology</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Due to the fact that we are varying assumptions in modeling as discussed above as well as using two different methods of fitting models to our data, the differences must be carefully interpreted. In Method A, we are creating a grid of best-fit solutions after each iteration of sampling from photometry. The results of this method rely heavily on the template set that we have generated\u2014specifically, how age is sampled, and what ionization or dust conditions are allowed. Taking only the best fit on each iteration may not take into account solutions that are nearly as good as the best-fit solution (e.g., the \"second best fit\"). In turn, this may affect our results by not including all \"good\" fits to the data. We believe this may be part of the reason that our results from Method A are, in general, less broad and sometimes bimodal, rather than a broader, unimodal distribution like those seen in Method B.</p><p>Method B uses nested sampling, which, similar to Markov Chain Monte Carlo techniques, is a robust way to calculate posteriors for distributions that may be multimodal or may have pronounced degeneracies. Rather than sampling from photometry, BAGPIPES explores an <em>n</em>-dimensional parameter space, each time judging the goodness of fit, and taking into account the input priors, outputs a marginalized posterior for all <em>n</em> parameters that are being fit. This means that generally, a smoother distribution of each parameter is found, and nearly all good solutions for the data are reported in the posteriors.</p><p>While these differences in methodology affect our results for individual galaxies, we note that the overall sample distributions (stellar mass, SFR, sSFR) do not change dramatically, with the exception of formation age (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5\">5</a>). This is likely because formation age is a difficult parameter to constrain, and due to our assumption of a constant SFH in Method A.</p></div></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s4\" name=\"apjabe533s4\">4.\u00a0Lens Modeling</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjabe533s4-1\" name=\"apjabe533s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Magnification Maps</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Many of our derived properties are unaffected by lensing, including age, dust extinction, metallicity, sSFR, and emission line strengths. Other properties (stellar mass, SFR, <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub>) must be corrected for lensing magnifications. To do so, we use magnification maps provided by RELICS lens modeling teams.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn8\">\n                  30\n               </a></sup>\n                The maps we utilize in this work are from one of three types of lens models: Lenstool (Jullo &amp; Kneib <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib48\">2009</a>), Glafic (Oguri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib70\">2010</a>), and light traces mass (LTM; e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib104\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib104\">2013</a>). Several papers have been published describing the lens models (Cerny et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib24\">2018</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib2\">2018</a>; Cibirka et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib26\">2018</a>; Paterno-Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib75\">2018</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib1\">2019</a>; Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib60\">2019</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib3\">2020</a>; Okabe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib71\">2020</a>). All models used in this work are available on MAST for public use. Not every model has been detailed in a publication, but the general methods are very similar to those described in the papers listed here.</p><p>For each method, a routine bootstrapping of lensing constraints is performed to create 50\u2013100 models, each yielding a magnification estimate given a lensed galaxy's position and redshift. We adopt the Method B median redshifts, extract the 50\u2013100 magnification estimates, then take the median as the estimate from each method. For clusters modeled by two or three methods, we take the average of those median estimates as our final magnification estimate for that galaxy.</p><p>The lens models provide magnification estimates for 150 of our 207 high-z candidates. The median of these 150 estimates is <em>\u03bc</em> = 2.9, and 127 (85%) of those have <em>\u03bc</em> &lt; 10. Only six have <em>\u03bc</em> &gt; 30, the highest being <em>\u03bc</em> = 96 for plckg287+32-2457, an average of 107 and 85 from glafic and LTM, respectively. We have reason to doubt individual magnification estimates of <em>\u03bc</em> &gt; 30 (Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib63\">2017</a>), but the average of medians lends somewhat more confidence to the few higher estimates reported here.</p><p>For some RELICS clusters, no strong lensing models are available, as no multiple images were identified in the HST imaging. In those cases, we adopt a nominal magnification of <em>\u03bc</em> = 3 for all high-<em>z</em> candidates. This is roughly the median of the 150 estimates and within a factor of 3 for 125 (83%) of those.</p><p>Given magnification estimates for all 207 high-z candidates, we divide our derived mass, SFR, and luminosity by these magnifications before reporting the results in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabe533t4\">4</a>. We also report the 68% confidence limits of each magnification estimate, but we do not add these to the reported uncertainties of the derived properties. This is so that the reader can easily use their own magnification and uncertainty measurements.</p><p>The models have varying numbers of multiple-image lensing constraints, making them not all equally reliable. For some clusters, no multiple images were identified for strong lensing analysis. In those cases, we relied on \"blind\" LTM models (in contrast with the regular LTM models mentioned above where constraints are available) with a mass-to-light normalization typical of other clusters (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib102\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib102\">2012</a>). For these blind LTM models, the (larger) uncertainties follow from the uncertainties in typical mass-to-light normalizations.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s4-2\" name=\"apjabe533s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0Source-plane Modeling</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We perform source-plane modeling for the 11 galaxies highlighted in this manuscript, and show the results in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f1\">1</a>. Closely following the methods of Yang et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib100\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib100\">2020</a>), we use the publicly available code <tt>Lenstruction</tt> (based on <tt>Lenstronomy</tt>\n               <sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn9\">\n                  31\n               </a></sup>\n               ) to forward model the high-<em>z</em> galaxies using their appearance in the image plane to predict their morphology and size in the source plane. <tt>Lenstruction</tt> takes into account distortion from lensing and the instrument PSF, and works to estimate pixel noise and remove the background flux level. We assume GLAFIC global magnification models when modeling each of the galaxies. We modeled each source as a singly-imaged galaxy, and we will explore this work further in Neufeld et al., in preparation.</p></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s5\" name=\"apjabe533s5\">5.\u00a0Results</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-1\" name=\"apjabe533s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Sample Selection</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The goal of this work is to explore the stellar properties of <em>z</em> \u2265 5.5 galaxies. We start from the sample defined in Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib82\">2020</a>), which consists of 321 HST-selected galaxy candidates with a best-fit <em>z</em> \u2265 5.5. Objects in this sample were required to have a median or peak in photometric redshift <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>) at <em>z</em> \u2265 5.5 in at least one of the redshift-fitting codes used, a &gt;3<em>\u03c3</em> detection in F160W, stellarity of &lt;8% (excluding point sources), and (Y\u2013J) color &gt;0.45 (both to filter out lower-redshift galaxies and brown dwarfs). The objects also passed an extensive visual inspection process where they were vetted for diffraction spikes, transients, detector edge noise, and other image artifacts (see Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib82\">2020</a> for more details).</p><p>We were able to successfully extract fluxes or flux upper limits in magnitude for 207 galaxies from Spitzer/IRAC observations using the process described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s2-2-2\">2.2.2</a>. The remaining 114 galaxies were too crowded by bright neighboring sources, usually cluster members. Most objects for which we could not extract a Spitzer/IRAC flux were within \u223c1'' of a cluster member or other bright (&lt;17 AB mag) galaxy. For a flux to be considered reliable, we require that the residual (model\u2014image) pixels be centered close to zero with the exception of artifacts from bright objects. The galaxies which were rejected generally showed a large over-subtraction or an obvious artifact in the residual.</p><p>Of the objects rejected from the sample, their apparent magnitudes, luminosities, and magnifications span a similar range as that of the entire sample. From this, we conclude that we are not biasing our sample by removing these galaxies.</p><p>Of the objects with reliable photometry, 96 have at least one IRAC detection of a signal-to-noise ratio (S/N) &gt; 1. The overall redshift distribution did not change significantly from the HST-only distribution (Figure 6 in Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib82\">2020</a>), though 23 galaxies now have significant peaks in redshift at <em>z</em> &lt; 4. In our analyses, we focus mostly on our final high-redshift candidate sample, but discuss the demoted objects further below.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-2\" name=\"apjabe533s5-2\">5.2.\u00a0Construction of the Catalog</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In the catalog<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn10\">\n                  32\n               </a></sup>\n               , we present the photometry of the 207 objects for which we successfully extracted Spitzer photometry, including HST fluxes and covariance indices from T-PHOT, which acts as a flag for possible blending with neighboring sources (galaxies with covariance indices <em>R</em><sub>[3.6],[4.5]</sub> &gt; 1 should be treated with caution, see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s2\">2</a> for the exact definitions). Along with photometry and covariance indices, we include median magnifications and 68% confidence limits from each lens model available on MAST, and the mean of those for any clusters with more than one model. In addition to photometry and magnification, we include the median values and upper and lower 68% confidence limits on redshift, SFR, stellar mass, sSFR, and age from our Methods A and B described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>. Units are described in the header of the catalog. We note that objects MACS0553-33-1014 and MACS0553-33-1016 were considered separate objects in Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib82\">2020</a>), but here we consider them the same galaxy, as their Spitzer/IRAC fluxes are blended and they are \u223c0.6'' apart. In the catalog, they are listed as object MACS0553-33-1014. All other objects have the same ID and coordinates as those in Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib82\">2020</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-3\" name=\"apjabe533s5-3\">5.3.\u00a0Properties of the Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In this section, we will first discuss the physical properties of the sample as a whole, presenting distributions of stellar mass, SFR, and age, and then later individual galaxies. Unless otherwise stated, we have used Method A for estimating the stellar properties presented here.</p><p>The quantity <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn104.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=PLrVNeC%2B0cxeWgWRJ1efbgx7oVg%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span>, the intrinsic luminosity of a source normalized by the characteristic luminosity for its redshift, informs the intrinsic brightness of our sample relative to that of the broader galaxy population. We calculate this by using <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn105.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=S7iWaseXvGRiRQO28QrFw3gB9q0%3D\" alt=\"${M}_{\\mathrm{UV}}=H160+2.5\\times {\\mathrm{log}}_{10}(1+z)-5\\times {\\mathrm{log}}_{10}({d}_{\\mathrm{pc}})+5$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{\\mathrm{UV}}=H160+2.5\\times {\\mathrm{log}}_{10}(1+z)-5\\times {\\mathrm{log}}_{10}({d}_{\\mathrm{pc}})+5</script></span></span>, where H160 is the observed F160W flux in AB magnitudes (chosen because all objects are detected significantly in this band, and <em>k</em>-corrections using this band are negligible), and <em>d</em><sub>pc</sub> is distance of the object in parsecs. We assume the characteristic magnitude of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn106.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=MUYkgnI0FIwuBjONr7ukEAF8MW4%3D\" alt=\"${M}_{\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }=(-20.95\\pm 0.10)\\,+(0.01\\pm 0.06)\\times ({z}_{\\mathrm{peak}}-6)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }=(-20.95\\pm 0.10)\\,+(0.01\\pm 0.06)\\times ({z}_{\\mathrm{peak}}-6)</script></span></span> from Bouwens et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib8\">2015</a>). We then correct for magnification in <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> and convert both <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn107.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=o%2FGt0LOZjeymQWAGsY8b2D8FDRM%3D\" alt=\"${M}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span> to luminosity. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f4\">4</a> we present the distribution of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn108.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=pKk3l5fPBgNk1lsjpm4IQE4WKE4%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span> for our sample, splitting the sample into those twice detected by IRAC, at least once detected, and the entire sample. \u223c95% of our sample falls at or below <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn109.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=SlMRCA2N%2BbwjXiUb2BuO58KlUUI%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span>, with a few objects <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn110.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=Er%2F1r7slMsKGzJ%2BGA4Pv3K9FLTY%3D\" alt=\"$\\sim 2{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sim 2{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span>, suggesting that we are probing a combination of galaxies characteristic for their redshifts as well as bright, perhaps unusual ones. The galaxies which are detected in both IRAC channels are, unsurprisingly, a larger percentage of intrinsically bright galaxies (the median value of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn111.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=QUhvb9z26gsrpOG4hdaO8IlKsdc%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span> for galaxies detected in both IRAC channels is 1.4, or \u221223.2 mag), however, we do detect several <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn112.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=qIoCpUO2cGcppI4dLsJoPaUD5FI%3D\" alt=\"$\\lt 0.5{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\lt 0.5{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span> galaxies.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn113.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=heeliapkL%2FUu8jZiQFeh2sb2jj4%3D\" A large majority of all three subsets of the sample are <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn114.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TSEiCCL4nwJdD15vTRMK%2FHlDWl8%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1</script></span></span>, with a few objects at.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=4lr1puxkc%2Blqy0UPuIeSBRut%2FzM%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f4_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=3YN9F5zMUqh51hO7Rg55SW7qksE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f5\">5</a>, we show distributions of best-fit age of formation (<em>t</em><sub>form</sub>), stellar mass, and SFR, using assumptions from Method A in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>. In each subplot, we again distinguish between the galaxies that are detected twice in IRAC, at least once in IRAC, and the entire sample. It is clear that galaxies in our sample with IRAC detections tend to take on a higher stellar mass, SFR, and formation age, possibly pointing to an observational bias. These are the same galaxies which tend toward intrinsically brighter (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn115.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=LQXR3rSIYyz6AZkMrk1AgLZ0%2FJs%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\gt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\gt 1</script></span></span>) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f4\">4</a>. We note that for a constant SFH, which we assume in our Method A SED fitting method, it is expected for at least some of these properties to be intrinsically correlated, as it will take a longer amount of time for a galaxy to build up a certain amount of mass at a given constant SFR. Notably, the overall distributions for the sample (and those of individual objects) between Methods A and B do not change appreciably, with the exception of formation age.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6SVPqCllXJNAvd7W%2F8aKovGtqSE%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6SVPqCllXJNAvd7W%2F8aKovGtqSE%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Histograms of best-fit physical parameters derived by Method A. In all panels, teal/blue is all 207 galaxies in the sample, tan is galaxies with at least one Spitzer/IRAC detection, and orange is galaxies with two Spitzer/IRAC detections. Left: best-fit log stellar mass distribution (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>). Detected galaxies fall on the higher stellar mass end, showing a bias of Spitzer being able to detect higher stellar mass objects due to brighter rest-frame optical fluxes. Center: best-fit log(SFR) distribution (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>/yr). Similar to stellar mass, higher SFR galaxies are more often detected than lower SFR (due to a correlation between <em>M</em>* and SFR). Right: best-fit log(age) distribution, where age here is age of formation in years: <em>t</em><sub>obs</sub>\u2013<em>t</em><sub>form</sub>. Galaxies detected in Spitzer tend to be older. Since we are assuming a constant SFH, this makes sense because older galaxies will have had more time to form more mass, an effect likely due to both the intrinsic properties of the galaxies we detect and our assumption of a constant SFH, which requires more massive galaxies to be older.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6SVPqCllXJNAvd7W%2F8aKovGtqSE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=YLtEKzEbAfVfmA577ghpga4SeIE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>As mentioned above, a possible effect at play in the sample distributions is an observational one: the galaxies with the brightest IRAC fluxes are the ones with detections, which will either be old galaxies with a strong Balmer break or young galaxies with strong emission lines. This is reflected in the age of formation distributions of each object in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f3\">3</a>. Within the sample of high-<em>z</em> candidates that received \u223c30 hr of Spitzer imaging, there are several examples of upper limits in flux constraining young galaxies (i.e., reaching depths of \u223c27 mag without seeing a detection and thus revealing a very blue spectral shape and pointing to a young stellar population absent of dust). However, a large majority of the time (because the majority of our sample contains relatively shallow, \u223c5 hr IRAC data), the comparison between detected and undetected galaxies is not necessarily a fair one. There could be galaxies with bright IRAC fluxes in clusters with shallow data that we are missing. Hence, the distributions in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f5\">5</a> show a combination of the variety of stellar populations we are probing with our sample and our observational limits.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f6\">6</a>, we show the Spitzer/IRAC [3.6]\u2013[4.5] color as a function of redshift for a variety of models, including a young (10 Myr) and dust-free template, and an evolved (500 Myr) dust-free template, as well as an evolved template with E(B-V) = 1.00. For comparison, we also include a Type 2 active galactic nucleus (AGN) model. These tracks were created from our set of BC03 + nebular continuum and emission templates described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a> (Method A). Plotted over these models, we show the objects in our data that have at least one IRAC detection of an S/N &gt; 3, highlighting select objects discussed below. Generally, we find that the IRAC colors in our sample trace the 10 and 500 Myr models without dust, with some notable exceptions which we discuss in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-4-1\">5.4.1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-4-3\">5.4.3</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TyXYqjrJrevWk%2BFH%2FTPyTQSZY0w%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TyXYqjrJrevWk%2BFH%2FTPyTQSZY0w%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0IRAC [3.6]\u2212[4.5] color vs. redshift for the galaxies in our sample with at least one IRAC S/N &gt; 3 detection (open circles with error bars). In red, brown, green, and blue open circles are PLCKG287+32-2013, RXC0911+14-143, A1763-1434, and ACT0102-49-2391, respectively, the objects we highlight later in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5\">5</a>. The dark blue filled square is MACS1149-JD from Hashimoto et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>), an object with evidence for an evolved stellar population at <em>z</em> = 9.11. Lines are tracks from various models from BC03, showing the predicted colors for that model. Redshifts are calculated independently from these models (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>). PLCKG287+32-2013 and ACT0102-49-2391 have colors consistent with the <em>z</em> = 6.6\u20136.9 color bump due to [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emission in [3.6]. A1763-1434 aligns well with older, dustier, and AGN models.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TyXYqjrJrevWk%2BFH%2FTPyTQSZY0w%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=ZQVRP%2F6zyHZZbf5XDAXtF3UjIco%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-4\" name=\"apjabe533s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0Detected Objects <em>z</em> \u2265 6.5</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>RELICS was designed to find and characterize galaxy populations at high redshift, apparently bright galaxies in a variety of fields that would be particularly good candidates for spectroscopic follow-up. For this reason, we highlight the objects we find to have the most informative data and be best candidates for follow-up. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f2\">2</a>, we show the best-fit Method A SEDs for the 11 galaxies in our sample that have at least one S/N &gt; 3 IRAC detection and a best-fit redshift of <em>z</em> \u2265 6.5. In blue, we show the best-fit high-redshift (<em>z</em> &gt; 4) template and in red, the best-fit low redshift template (<em>z</em> &lt; 4) using the Method A assumptions described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>. The posteriors of stellar mass, formation age, SFR, sSFR, and formation time for individual objects from Method A are shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f3\">3</a>. In yellow/tan filled histograms, we show the results from Method A (BC03 templates, constant SFH), and in open dark green histograms, we show the results of Method B (BPASS, rising/declining SFH defined by Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjabe533eqn1\">1</a>), extra birth cloud dust).</p><p>Of note in this sample is that several of the objects have likely redshifts around <em>z</em> \u223c 6.6\u20137 because of an elevated [3.6] magnitude, which indicates a strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emission line falling in ch1 at those redshifts. Additionally, we also see several objects that prefer a nearly maximally old formation age; that is, the galaxies would have started forming &lt;100 Myr after the big bang.</p><h4 id=\"apjabe533s5-4-1\" name=\"apjabe533s5-4-1\">5.4.1.\u00a0Objects with Potentially Strong Emission Lines</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Of the objects highlighted in this work, the ones with blue IRAC [3.6]\u2013[4.5] colors at <em>z</em> = 6.8\u20137.0 include ACT0102-49-2391, A1758-1942, PLCKG287+32-698, PLCKG287+32-777, and PLCKG287+32-2013. Of these, we focus on three objects in PLCKG287+32: 2013, 698, and 777, as well as ACT0102-40-2391.</p><p>We show our analysis of these objects with BAGPIPES in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f7\">7</a>: SEDs fit to the median and \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> in mass-weighted age template (note that we are now using mass-weighted age, defined in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjabe533eqn3\">3</a>) instead of formation age as it is more easily constrained), and the median \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> in mass-weighted age SFHs in corresponding colors, as well as all allowed SFHs in gray. In PLCKG287+32-2013, one can see that the youngest (blue line) template shows a very recent, steeply rising burst of star formation. With a lower S/N detection, the range of solutions and SFHs for ACT0102-49-2391 are much more dramatic.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=qYaHu00c0KWjWtGLQp6GeRU3w7Q%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f7a\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=qYaHu00c0KWjWtGLQp6GeRU3w7Q%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f7a\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=WN5bi8gwGpY6600LwDmzCbNNc3o%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden;
