A large majority of all three subsets of the sample are <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn114.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TSEiCCL4nwJdD15vTRMK%2FHlDWl8%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1</script></span></span>, with a few objects at.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=4lr1puxkc%2Blqy0UPuIeSBRut%2FzM%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f4_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=3YN9F5zMUqh51hO7Rg55SW7qksE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f5\">5</a>, we show distributions of best-fit age of formation (<em>t</em><sub>form</sub>), stellar mass, and SFR, using assumptions from Method A in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>. In each subplot, we again distinguish between the galaxies that are detected twice in IRAC, at least once in IRAC, and the entire sample. It is clear that galaxies in our sample with IRAC detections tend to take on a higher stellar mass, SFR, and formation age, possibly pointing to an observational bias. These are the same galaxies which tend toward intrinsically brighter (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn115.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=LQXR3rSIYyz6AZkMrk1AgLZ0%2FJs%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\gt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\gt 1</script></span></span>) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f4\">4</a>. We note that for a constant SFH, which we assume in our Method A SED fitting method, it is expected for at least some of these properties to be intrinsically correlated, as it will take a longer amount of time for a galaxy to build up a certain amount of mass at a given constant SFR. Notably, the overall distributions for the sample (and those of individual objects) between Methods A and B do not change appreciably, with the exception of formation age.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6SVPqCllXJNAvd7W%2F8aKovGtqSE%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6SVPqCllXJNAvd7W%2F8aKovGtqSE%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0Histograms of best-fit physical parameters derived by Method A. In all panels, teal/blue is all 207 galaxies in the sample, tan is galaxies with at least one Spitzer/IRAC detection, and orange is galaxies with two Spitzer/IRAC detections. Left: best-fit log stellar mass distribution (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>). Detected galaxies fall on the higher stellar mass end, showing a bias of Spitzer being able to detect higher stellar mass objects due to brighter rest-frame optical fluxes. Center: best-fit log(SFR) distribution (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>/yr). Similar to stellar mass, higher SFR galaxies are more often detected than lower SFR (due to a correlation between <em>M</em>* and SFR). Right: best-fit log(age) distribution, where age here is age of formation in years: <em>t</em><sub>obs</sub>\u2013<em>t</em><sub>form</sub>. Galaxies detected in Spitzer tend to be older. Since we are assuming a constant SFH, this makes sense because older galaxies will have had more time to form more mass, an effect likely due to both the intrinsic properties of the galaxies we detect and our assumption of a constant SFH, which requires more massive galaxies to be older.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=6SVPqCllXJNAvd7W%2F8aKovGtqSE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f5\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f5_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=YLtEKzEbAfVfmA577ghpga4SeIE%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>As mentioned above, a possible effect at play in the sample distributions is an observational one: the galaxies with the brightest IRAC fluxes are the ones with detections, which will either be old galaxies with a strong Balmer break or young galaxies with strong emission lines. This is reflected in the age of formation distributions of each object in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f3\">3</a>. Within the sample of high-<em>z</em> candidates that received \u223c30 hr of Spitzer imaging, there are several examples of upper limits in flux constraining young galaxies (i.e., reaching depths of \u223c27 mag without seeing a detection and thus revealing a very blue spectral shape and pointing to a young stellar population absent of dust). However, a large majority of the time (because the majority of our sample contains relatively shallow, \u223c5 hr IRAC data), the comparison between detected and undetected galaxies is not necessarily a fair one. There could be galaxies with bright IRAC fluxes in clusters with shallow data that we are missing. Hence, the distributions in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f5\">5</a> show a combination of the variety of stellar populations we are probing with our sample and our observational limits.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f6\">6</a>, we show the Spitzer/IRAC [3.6]\u2013[4.5] color as a function of redshift for a variety of models, including a young (10 Myr) and dust-free template, and an evolved (500 Myr) dust-free template, as well as an evolved template with E(B-V) = 1.00. For comparison, we also include a Type 2 active galactic nucleus (AGN) model. These tracks were created from our set of BC03 + nebular continuum and emission templates described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a> (Method A). Plotted over these models, we show the objects in our data that have at least one IRAC detection of an S/N &gt; 3, highlighting select objects discussed below. Generally, we find that the IRAC colors in our sample trace the 10 and 500 Myr models without dust, with some notable exceptions which we discuss in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-4-1\">5.4.1</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-4-3\">5.4.3</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TyXYqjrJrevWk%2BFH%2FTPyTQSZY0w%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TyXYqjrJrevWk%2BFH%2FTPyTQSZY0w%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0IRAC [3.6]\u2212[4.5] color vs. redshift for the galaxies in our sample with at least one IRAC S/N &gt; 3 detection (open circles with error bars). In red, brown, green, and blue open circles are PLCKG287+32-2013, RXC0911+14-143, A1763-1434, and ACT0102-49-2391, respectively, the objects we highlight later in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5\">5</a>. The dark blue filled square is MACS1149-JD from Hashimoto et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>), an object with evidence for an evolved stellar population at <em>z</em> = 9.11. Lines are tracks from various models from BC03, showing the predicted colors for that model. Redshifts are calculated independently from these models (see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>). PLCKG287+32-2013 and ACT0102-49-2391 have colors consistent with the <em>z</em> = 6.6\u20136.9 color bump due to [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emission in [3.6]. A1763-1434 aligns well with older, dustier, and AGN models.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=TyXYqjrJrevWk%2BFH%2FTPyTQSZY0w%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f6_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=ZQVRP%2F6zyHZZbf5XDAXtF3UjIco%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-4\" name=\"apjabe533s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0Detected Objects <em>z</em> \u2265 6.5</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>RELICS was designed to find and characterize galaxy populations at high redshift, apparently bright galaxies in a variety of fields that would be particularly good candidates for spectroscopic follow-up. For this reason, we highlight the objects we find to have the most informative data and be best candidates for follow-up. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f2\">2</a>, we show the best-fit Method A SEDs for the 11 galaxies in our sample that have at least one S/N &gt; 3 IRAC detection and a best-fit redshift of <em>z</em> \u2265 6.5. In blue, we show the best-fit high-redshift (<em>z</em> &gt; 4) template and in red, the best-fit low redshift template (<em>z</em> &lt; 4) using the Method A assumptions described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>. The posteriors of stellar mass, formation age, SFR, sSFR, and formation time for individual objects from Method A are shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f3\">3</a>. In yellow/tan filled histograms, we show the results from Method A (BC03 templates, constant SFH), and in open dark green histograms, we show the results of Method B (BPASS, rising/declining SFH defined by Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjabe533eqn1\">1</a>), extra birth cloud dust).</p><p>Of note in this sample is that several of the objects have likely redshifts around <em>z</em> \u223c 6.6\u20137 because of an elevated [3.6] magnitude, which indicates a strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emission line falling in ch1 at those redshifts. Additionally, we also see several objects that prefer a nearly maximally old formation age; that is, the galaxies would have started forming &lt;100 Myr after the big bang.</p><h4 id=\"apjabe533s5-4-1\" name=\"apjabe533s5-4-1\">5.4.1.\u00a0Objects with Potentially Strong Emission Lines</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Of the objects highlighted in this work, the ones with blue IRAC [3.6]\u2013[4.5] colors at <em>z</em> = 6.8\u20137.0 include ACT0102-49-2391, A1758-1942, PLCKG287+32-698, PLCKG287+32-777, and PLCKG287+32-2013. Of these, we focus on three objects in PLCKG287+32: 2013, 698, and 777, as well as ACT0102-40-2391.</p><p>We show our analysis of these objects with BAGPIPES in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f7\">7</a>: SEDs fit to the median and \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> in mass-weighted age template (note that we are now using mass-weighted age, defined in Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjabe533eqn3\">3</a>) instead of formation age as it is more easily constrained), and the median \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> in mass-weighted age SFHs in corresponding colors, as well as all allowed SFHs in gray. In PLCKG287+32-2013, one can see that the youngest (blue line) template shows a very recent, steeply rising burst of star formation. With a lower S/N detection, the range of solutions and SFHs for ACT0102-49-2391 are much more dramatic.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabe533f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabe533f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=qYaHu00c0KWjWtGLQp6GeRU3w7Q%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=qYaHu00c0KWjWtGLQp6GeRU3w7Q%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f7a\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=qYaHu00c0KWjWtGLQp6GeRU3w7Q%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f7a\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7a_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=WN5bi8gwGpY6600LwDmzCbNNc3o%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7b_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=n5FaoZfig9oxbAR9CCpARDGctz4%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0Left: observed photometry (black), model photometry (open squares), and SEDs of the objects discussed in Sections <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-4-1\">5.4.1</a> and <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s5-4-3\">5.4.3</a> from Method B using BAGPIPES. Yellow template is an SED with the median mass-weighted age, blue is 1<em>\u03c3</em> younger, and red is 1<em>\u03c3</em> older. See Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3-3\">3.3</a> for distinction between formation age and mass-weighted age. Right: SFHs of the templates on the left, in corresponding colors. Circles denote times of mass-weighted ages. In gray are all SFH realizations.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabe533f7b\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7b_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=n5FaoZfig9oxbAR9CCpARDGctz4%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabe533f7b\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533f7b_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=F9E3FCtJNRTdUhBAMdMA7xWbnME%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Additionally, we highlight these objects in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f6\">6</a>. Of the four galaxies, PLCKG287+32-2013 has the highest S/N [3.6] detection (\u223c9<em>\u03c3</em>). EA<em>z</em>Y yields <em>z</em> = 7.3 \u00b1 0.3, while BAGPIPES finds <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn116.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=CkOeR%2BNEtISniw8asbVtRtWdl2o%3D\" alt=\"$z={6.8}_{-0.1}^{+0.2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">z={6.8}_{-0.1}^{+0.2}</script></span></span>. As can be seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f6\">6</a> (red and light blue circles), both PLCKG287+32-2013 and ACT0102-49-2391 show more extreme (blue) IRAC colors than most of the sample. In combination with the fact that they are consistent with the expected color decrease at <em>z</em> \u223c 6.6\u20136.9 for young, star-forming galaxies, we believe these are potentially strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emitters. The PLCKG287+32-2013 IRAC color corresponds to rest-frame EWs of \u223c1000 \u00c5. Compared to galaxies observed at <em>z</em> = 0.2\u20130.9 where [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> EWs are commonly observed to be &lt;100\u00c5 and extreme emission line galaxies are defined to be anything above that (Mainali et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib61\">2020</a>; Amor\u00edn et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib4\">2014</a>), this is a relatively high EW, and in line with the extreme emitters discovered by Endsley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib33\">2021</a>; 1000\u20134000 \u00c5 at <em>z</em> \u223c 7) and those discovered by Mainali et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib61\">2020</a>; 500\u20132000 \u00c5 at <em>z</em> \u223c 2\u22123). These high EWs suggest a high value of the ionization parameter log(U), which corresponds to a hard spectrum and an ISM or even circumgalactic medium replete with energetic ionizing photons. One possible consequence of this suggestion is the presence of an ionized bubble carved out by star formation (see, e.g., Tilvi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib97\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib97\">2020</a>) as a result of an ongoing burst of star formation (consistent with the youngest SFH for PLCKG287+32-2013 in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f7\">7</a>). Endsley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib33\">2021</a>) argued that while galaxies with extreme [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> may be experiencing a short-lived burst of star formation that, based on similarities to Lyman-continuum leakers (galaxies that \"leak\" Lyman-continuum photons) at <em>z</em> \u223c 3, these objects tend to have very high escape fractions at least for the duration of the burst. Additionally, since these galaxies seem to make up \u223c20% of the <em>z</em> \u223c 7 population (though with large uncertainties), together they might be among the most effective objects at ionizing the IGM.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn11\">\n                     33\n                  </a></sup>\n                  \n               </p><p>Two of these objects (PLCKG287+32-698 and 777) are multiple images in a quadruply imaged system, first discovered by Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib105\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib105\">2017</a>). In addition to finding the same redshift for both images analyzed here, we find convincingly similar physical properties with both methods, as can be seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f3\">3</a> (M<sub>*</sub> \u223c 10<sup>8\u22129</sup>\n                  <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, sSFR \u223c 10 Gyr<sup>\u22121</sup>). We note the third lensed image in this system, PLCKG287+32-2235, is included in our catalog but is not significantly detected in Spitzer. The fourth lensed image PLCKG287+32-2977 was discovered in WFC3/IR F110W imaging but lands outside RELICS imaging in other WFC3/IR filters and was thus excluded from the Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib81\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib81\">2018</a>) selection and this work.</p><p>The last object we focus on in this section, PLCKG287+32-2013, yields some similarities to two galaxies confirmed by ALMA with [C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] by Smit et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib90\">2018</a>). They are of similar observed brightness (apparent magnitude \u223c24.9), similar SFRs (within the uncertainties), and have similar IRAC colors. Smit et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib90\">2018</a>) argued that an elevated [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> EW suggests a higher [C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] EW, potentially meaning a more likely detection with ALMA follow-up for these objects. This would allow us to place them on the IR excess-UV slope (IRX-<em>\u03b2</em>) relation. Additionally, such extreme EWs are indicative of potential Ly-<em>\u03b1</em> emission (Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib78\">2016</a>; Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib69\">2015</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib103\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib103\">2015</a>; Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib93\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib93\">2017</a>), and [C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (e.g., Hutchison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib46\">2019</a>) presenting opportunities for ground-based IR spectroscopy.</p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s5-4-2\" name=\"apjabe533s5-4-2\">5.4.2.\u00a0Gemini Observations of PLCKG287+32-777</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We take this opportunity to note that PLCKG287+32-777 was spectroscopically observed with Gemini South (GS-2018A-Q-901; PI: Adi Zitrin), and we publish here the first results from these observations. It was first observed for 2 hr with Flamingos-2 (R3000 Grism + <em>J</em>-band filter). A 4-pixel (0.72'') wide longslit was used, leading to an average resolving power of <em>R</em> \u223c 1300, with a nod size of \u00b11.5'' These observations were designed to detect potential  <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> emission, although the detection limit is quite high (e.g., a 3<em>\u03c3</em> limit per spectral pixel of \u2243 1\u20131.5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221216</sup>erg/cm<sup>2</sup>/\u00c5 for a spectral line width of 100\u2013300 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>).</p><p>The object was also observed with Gemini Multi-Object Spectrograph (GMOS) in longslit Nod &amp; Shuffle mode, with a 1'' wide slit and a \u00b12.5'' nod size. Observations took place using the R400 grism + <em>z</em>-band filter, and two different central wavelengths (760 nm, 795 nm), for \u22434.3 hr. These observations were aimed to detect Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, and have a nominal 3<em>\u03c3</em> depth per spectral pixel of 4.5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>\u221217</sup>erg/cm<sup>2</sup>/\u00c5 for a 500 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Ly-<em>\u03b1</em> line at <em>z</em> = 6.8. At higher redshifts, the sensitivity drops, reaching half the supernova remnant for Ly-<em>\u03b1</em> at <em>z</em> \u2243 7.3.</p><p>Both the F-2 and GMOS longslits also covered a second image of the lensed galaxy (2977), however, the nod size in the GMOS observations only allowed for a credible search around the first object. No prominent emission line was detected in either observation for either image, suggesting that Ly-<em>\u03b1</em> may not be easily escaping this galaxy and the presence of extremely strong  <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> emission is likely ruled out. However, we note that the data merit a more careful inspection, and we leave a more quantitative examination of these data for future work.</p></div><h4 id=\"apjabe533s5-4-3\" name=\"apjabe533s5-4-3\">5.4.3.\u00a0Objects Preferring Maximally Old Formation Age</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In addition to galaxies with potentially strong nebular emission, there are a subset of galaxies in our sample that show a strong preference to Method A and some preference to Method B, for a maximally old solution, with the earliest possible formation time, including ACT0102-49-2551, RXC0911+17-143, MACS0553-33-219, A1763-1434, and CL0152-13-191. Here we focus on A1763-1434 and RXC0911+17-143, both of which were initially introduced by Strait et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib96\">2020</a>). The former has since received deeper data (\u223c30 hr, from \u223c5 hr in Strait et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib96\">2020</a>).</p><p>As has been noted previously in this work, formation age is a difficult parameter to constrain, since the beginning of SFHs tend to leave little imprint on a photometric SED. The results tend to be reliant on the implicit prior of the assumed SFH, which in the case of our Method A is constant. In Method B, we report mass-weighted age instead, since this quantity can be more reliably fit for. We will discuss both here.</p><p>In the fifth panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f7\">7</a>, we show our analysis with BAGPIPES of A1763-1434, where we plot again median \u00b11<em>\u03c3</em> mass-weighted age templates. In all cases, these templates represent relatively evolved stellar populations, reflected by the SFHs on the right panel that \"turn on\" relatively early. The oldest solution prefers a gently declining SFH, while the others are relatively constant. There are some solutions that prefer a high [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> EW (i.e., a young, high sSFR template rather than an evolved one with a Balmer break), which falls in IRAC ch2 at the redshift of this galaxy (<em>z</em> \u223c 8.4), but overwhelmingly, an evolved stellar population is preferred.</p><p>We see a similar case in RXC0911+17-143, shown on the bottom panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f7\">7</a>: this time with both IRAC fluxes elevated, there is evidence of a Balmer break, although the detections are a lower S/N compared to A1763. The SFHs reflect a similar preference for a declining or constant, but relatively stagnant SFH for this object. When these objects are compared to the rest of our sample in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f6\">6</a>, both are consistent with an evolved and/or dusty solution. The red IRAC colors make these objects stand out from the rest of our sample as being closer in color-redshift space to MACS1149-JD, a spectroscopically confirmed galaxy with evidence for an early formation time (Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>).</p><p>Similarly, ACT0102-49-2551, CL0152-13-191, and MACS0553-33-219 show evidence of a Balmer break. In the cases of these galaxies, both IRAC fluxes are elevated, making it less likely that the elevated flux is due to emission lines alone. The EW of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H<em>\u03b2</em> EW for MACS055-33-219 and ACT0102-49-2551 would need to be \u223c1000 and \u223c300 \u00c5, respectively. In the case of CL0152-13-191, the Spitzer fluxes are elevated by \u223c2.3 mag, and we do not find any well-fit young solutions in either method. Further follow-up on these galaxies will be necessary for proper modeling.</p><p>As argued by Strait et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib96\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib96\">2020</a>) and Roberts-Borsani et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>), IRAC excess in [4.5], while often attributed to high values of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> (which fall in ch2 at <em>z</em> &gt; 7), can be equally (and sometimes more favorably) explained by a strong Balmer break, suggesting an evolved stellar population and/or dust. Roberts-Borsani et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>; and previously Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>) go on to suggest ways of distinguishing these solutions, including the use of the ALMA to detect the [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] 88<em> \u03bc</em>m line, whose strength is related to the [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] 5007 \u00c5 line. We suspect that the galaxies discussed in this section likely have Balmer breaks and evolved stellar populations.</p></div></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-5\" name=\"apjabe533s5-5\">5.5.\u00a0SFHs and Age Constraints</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>In addition to the Method B BAGPIPES run described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabe533s3\">3</a>, we also performed an alternative run for the 11 galaxies highlighted in this paper, in order to explore a wider parameter space and test the effect of our assumptions on the difference in results between Methods A and B. For this run, we allowed redshifts to range from <em>z</em> = 0\u201312, adopted an SMC dust law, and allowed for a freely varying constant SFH and a recent burst (as in, e.g., Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>; Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>). The effect that these changes had on the candidates described here was mainly due to the newly allowed burst in the SFHs. In some cases, this increased the median age, and in some cases, it lowered the median age. Our conclusion from this test is that our data are unable to meaningfully constrain the SFHs of this sample. This is also evident in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabe533f7\">7</a>, where it is clear that there are many SFH solutions that fit the data. We plan to explore what data would be necessary to constrain SFHs meaningfully, including testing JWST and ALMA data, in a future work.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabe533s5-6\" name=\"apjabe533s5-6\">5.6.\u00a0Demoted Objects</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Of the 207 galaxies for which we could extract reliable Spitzer fluxes, 23 have nontrivial peaks in redshift at <em>z</em> &lt; 2, revealing a \u223c10% contamination rate for HST-selected Lyman-break galaxies. Notably, the bright <em>z</em> \u223c 6 galaxies behind RXS0603+42 (both north and south HST pointings) had a high \"failure\" rate. Of the 13 high-redshift candidates behind this cluster, we were able to extract Spitzer fluxes for eight. Of those, only two remain likely at high redshift. The rest are likely <em>z</em> \u223c 1 galaxies or brown dwarfs.</p></div></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s6\" name=\"apjabe533s6\">6.\u00a0Future Data</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Ultimately, spectroscopic follow-up will be necessary to place strong constraints on the properties of these galaxies, such as dust content, metal enrichment, and ionization field. There are a number of existing and future telescopes that will be well suited to this task.</p><p>Ground-based IR spectroscopy with telescopes such as Keck will allow for a search of rest-frame UV emission lines such as Ly<em>\u03b1</em> and [C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]. There are a number of studies detailing how such observations can aid in constraining SFR, metallicities, AGN activity, and ionization with these emission lines (e.g., Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib41\">2013</a>; Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib93\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib93\">2017</a>; Nakajima et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib67\">2018</a>; Hutchison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib46\">2019</a>; Le F\u00e8vre et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib54\">2020</a>). As discussed above, the presence of strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emission makes detection of Ly<em>\u03b1</em> and [C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] more likely.</p><p>A millimeter/submillimeter telescope such as ALMA could be used to determine the presence of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] 88<em> \u03bc</em>m or [C <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] 158<em> \u03bc</em>m, which would lead to insight on the dust and metal content (e.g., Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>; Brada\u010d et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib11\">2017</a>), constraints on the strength of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] 5007 \u00c5 (Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib43\">2018</a>; Roberts-Borsani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib79\">2020</a>), and even kinematics (e.g., Smit et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib90\">2018</a>). Placing high-redshift galaxies on the IRX-<em>\u03b2</em> relation is valuable for understanding typical dust properties relative to the shape of the UV continuum. We calculate the UV slopes<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn12\">\n               34\n            </a></sup>\n             of PLCKG287+32-2013 and ACT0102-49-2391 from the photometry to be \u22121.7 \u00b1 1.1 and \u22121.7 \u00b1 1.3, respectively. For the <em>\u03b2</em> slopes of these values, following the Meurer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib66\">1999</a> relation for local galaxies, one would expect values of IRX (log(<em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>/<em>L</em><sub><em>m</em>UV</sub>)) of around \u223c0\u20131. However, there has been high scatter observed in this relation, and in fact the <em>z</em> &gt; 5 galaxies studied by, e.g., Willott et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib98\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib98\">2015</a>); Capak et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib19\">2015</a>); Knudsen et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib50\">2017</a>); Smit et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib90\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib90\">2018</a>); Fudamoto et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabe533bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjabe533bib42\">2020</a>) are found to have a lower-than-expected IRX. This decrement is still present even when assuming a steep attenuation law such as SMC, perhaps explained by a higher dust temperature at high redshift.</p><p>With capabilities out to 28 microns, JWST will revolutionize high-redshift galaxy spectroscopy and imaging and allow us to do detailed analyses of the rest-frame UV and optical spectrum for galaxies in the <em>z</em> \u2273 6 regime. Notably, spectroscopic (and inferred photometric) measurements of [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> and their strength relative to other rest-frame UV and rest-frame optical emission lines will aid in our understanding of ionization field, ionizing photon production, oxygen abundance, and sSFR.</p></div><h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabe533s7\" name=\"apjabe533s7\">7.\u00a0Conclusions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We present an analysis of new Spitzer/IRAC imaging for the high-<em>z</em> candidates in the RELICS survey, providing a full HST and Spitzer catalog, with galaxy properties, redshifts, and magnifications for 207 galaxies likely at <em>z</em> \u2265 5.5. We present the distributions of stellar properties of the sample using templates from BC03 and BPASS + nebular emission, and highlight 11 galaxies that have the highest redshifts (<em>z</em> \u2265 6.5) and at least one S/N detection &gt;3 in IRAC. We go into further detail for six of those objects. Our main conclusions are as follows:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">While \u223c95% of our sample are characteristic for their redshift (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn117.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=JpQhcqQyqh9HgiP898Ty1T3tt9Q%3D\" alt=\"${L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }\\lt 1</script></span></span>), there are a few that are intrinsically brighter (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/910/2/135/1/apjabe533ieqn118.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1636998009&amp;Signature=lXhitWkWTb9T1I9fOy1YLVokVyc%3D\" alt=\"$\\sim 2{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sim 2{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}/{L}_{m\\mathrm{UV}}^{* }</script></span></span>). Within our sample, we see a variety of stellar populations, from very small at 2.1 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>5 </sup>\n                     <em>M</em><sub>*</sub> to very massive at 4.2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>9</sup> \n                     <em>M</em><sub>*</sub>, and from very young (forming &gt;800 Myr after the big bang) to very old (forming &lt;100 Myr after the big bang).</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Along with this paper, we are releasing our full HST + Spitzer/IRAC photometric catalog<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabe533fn13\">\n                        35\n                     </a></sup>\n                     , as well as results from both methods of SED fitting described in this work and magnifications from publicly available lens models.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We find that PLCKG287+32-2013, one of the brightest <em>z</em> \u223c 7 candidates known (F160W mag 24.9), has strong evidence for strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]+H-<em>\u03b2</em> emission (EW \u223c 1000 \u00c5), as suggested by its elevated Spitzer [3.6] flux. We believe that this galaxy is experiencing an ongoing burst of star formation.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We find a similar object, ACT0102-49-2391, which although less luminous, also reveals an elevated [3.6] flux, and falls around <em>z</em> \u223c 6.6\u22126.9, again suggesting strong nebular emission.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">5.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We find two objects, A1763-1434 and RXC0911+17-143, that show evidence for an evolved stellar population, i.e., that they formed very early (&lt;100 Myr after the big bang). We believe that these galaxies show good evidence of Balmer breaks, and their IRAC colors are consistent with evolved (500 Myr) or dusty (E(B-V) = 1.00) galaxy models.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">6.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">While several of our <em>z</em> \u2265 6.5 galaxies which are detected in IRAC prefer a nearly maximally old solution, there may be other explanations for their bright Spitzer fluxes, such as dust or extreme line emission. Disentangling these degeneracies will only be possible with, e.g., JWST, Keck, Thirty Meter Telescope, and/or ALMA observations.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">7.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Through the exploration of SFHs, we find that formation age, which is commonly used as the measure of age in high-redshift galaxy studies, is difficult to constrain. We report it here for comparison with other works, but also present mass-weighted age, a more easily constrained property.</div></li></ul><p>\n         </p><p>The galaxies presented in this work will be excellent targets for follow-up existing telescopes like Keck and ALMA, as well as with future telescopes such as James Webb, the Thirty Meter Telescope, and the Giant Magellan Telescope, as they are apparently bright but intrinsically faint, and likely the dominant galaxy population at these epochs.</p></div><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>This work is based on observations made with the Spitzer Space Telescope, which is operated by the Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology under a contract with NASA. It is also based on observations made with the NASA/ESA Hubble Space Telescope, obtained at the Space Telescope Science Institute, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., under NASA contract NAS 5-26555. Support for this work was provided by NASA through ADAP grant 80NSSC18K0945, NSF grant AST 1815458, NASA/HST grant HST-GO-14096, and through an award issued by JPL/Caltech. V.S. also acknowledges support through Heising-Simons Foundation grant no. 2018-1140.</p><p>This work also refers to observations obtained at the international Gemini Observatory, a program of NSF's OIR Lab, which is managed by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy (AURA) under a cooperative agreement with the National Science Foundation on behalf of the Gemini Observatory partnership: the National Science Foundation (United States), National Research Council (Canada), Agencia Nacional de Investigaci\u00f3n y Desarrollo (Chile), Ministerio de Ciencia, Tecnolog\u00eda e Innovaci\u00f3n (Argentina), Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia, Tecnologia, Inova\u00e7\u00f5es e Comunica\u00e7\u00f5es (Brazil), and Korea Astronomy and Space Science Institute (Republic of Korea). The guaranteed observations used here were obtained through Ben-Gurion University's (BGU; Israel) time on Gemini, following an memorandum of understanding between BGU and Gemini/AURA.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"clear-list
